reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs a transposed convolution, multiplies by a scalar, applies global average pooling, 
    another global average pooling
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, multiplier):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding)
        self.multiplier = multiplier

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv_transpose(x)
        x = x * self.multiplier
        x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2, 3], keepdim=True)  # First global average pooling
        x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2, 3], keepdim=True)  # Second global average pooling
        return x

batch_size = 16
in_channels = 64
out_channels = 128
height, width = 128, 128
kernel_size = 3
stride = 2
padding = 1
output_padding = 1
multiplier = 0.5

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, multiplier]

The task is to write an optimized CUDA kernel that replaces the forward pass of a PyTorch model. The reference model performs: 1. `ConvTranspose2d` (also called deconvolution) 2. So the second `mean` is idempotent. Actually, after first mean, spatial dims are 1, so second mean gives same value. So overall, the forward is equivalent to `mean(conv_transpose(x) * multiplier, dim=(2,3), keepdim=True)`. Actually second mean returns same because a mean of a single element is itself. Wait: mean over dims [2,3] after first mean (which produced [B, C, 1, 1]) yields [B, C, 1, 1] same values. So second mean doesn't change values. But the output shape is [batch_size, out_channels, 1, 1]. So the whole forward pass reduces to computing the global average pooling of the transposed convolution output times multiplier. We can optimize this by fusing the transposed convolution with the reduction. Instead of materializing the full transposed conv output (which could be large), compute each output element on the fly and accumulate into per-channel sums. For transposed convolution (stride=2, kernel_size=3, padding=1, output_padding=1), the output spatial size is: H_out = (H_in - 1)*stride - 2*padding + kernel_size + output_padding = (128-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 + 1 = 254 - 2 + 4 = 256. So output shape is [16, 128, 256, 256] which is 16*128*256*256 = 134,217,728 floats ≈ 512 MB. That's large. Computing the transposed conv and then reducing over huge memory is memory-bound. Fusing them saves a lot of memory bandwidth. We need to implement a custom CUDA kernel. At 20 TFLOPS, that's 0.1 seconds, maybe okay but not ideal. However, most of those are wasted if we treat it as conv; a proper conv algorithm uses im2col or Winograd to reuse loads. For fp32, memory bandwidth is maybe 1-2 TB/s. Reading 512 MB takes ~0.5 ms. Computing the transposed conv itself might take more, maybe 1-2 ms. The custom kernel must beat the reference. The reference likely uses PyTorch's optimized ConvTranspose2d (cuDNN) + elementwise multiply + reduction kernels. The reduction is cheap. The heavy part is the conv transpose. Can we speed up by fusing? Maybe not dramatically in compute time, but we can save the memory bandwidth of writing and reading the 512 MB output. However, the conv transpose compute is likely memory-bound as well, but it's still a lot of compute. If we compute the mean directly from the conv transpose without storing the full output, we avoid writing 512 MB and reading it back for the reduction. That's a significant bandwidth saving. But implementing a highly efficient transposed conv from scratch in CUDA is difficult to beat cuDNN. However, since we only need the spatial sum, we might be able to precompute something to reduce the compute drastically. Let's revisit the math: We need S[b, oc] = sum_{oh,ow} out[b, oc, oh, ow]. S[b, oc] = sum_{ic=0}^{C_in-1} sum_{ih=0}^{H_in-1} sum_{iw=0}^{W_in-1} x[b,ic,ih,iw] * (sum_{kh,kw valid for (ih,iw)} weight[ic,oc,kh,kw]) + bias[oc] * H_out * W_out. Let W_eff[ic, oc, ih, iw] = sum_{kh,kw valid} weight[ic, oc, kh, kw]. Since the validity pattern depends only on whether ih=0 (top) or iw=0 (left). For ih>0 and iw>0, all kh,kw are valid, so W_eff = sum_{kh,kw} weight. For ih=0, iw>0: valid kh are {1,2} (assuming kh=0 invalid). So W_eff = sum_{kh=1..2, kw=0..2} weight. For ih>0, iw=0: valid kw are {1,2}. So W_eff = sum_{kh=0..2, kw=1..2} weight. For ih=0, iw=0: valid kh={1,2}, kw={1,2}. So W_eff = sum_{kh=1..2, kw=1..2} weight. So W_eff takes only 4 possible values per (ic,oc): corner (0,0), top edge (0, iw>0), left edge (ih>0, 0), interior. Thus S[b,oc] = sum_{ic} [ x[b,ic,0,0] * W_corner[ic,oc] + sum_{iw=1}^{W_in-1} x[b,ic,0,iw] * W_top[ic,oc] + sum_{ih=1}^{H_in-1} x[b,ic,ih,0] * W_left[ic,oc] + sum_{ih=1}^{H_in-1} sum_{iw=1}^{W_in-1} x[b,ic,ih,iw] * W_full[ic,oc] ] + bias*H_out*W_out. Where: W_full[ic,oc] = sum_{kh=0..2, kw=0..2} weight[ic,oc,kh,kw]. W_top[ic,oc] = sum_{kh=1..2, kw=0..2} weight[ic,oc,kh,kw]. W_left[ic,oc] = sum_{kh=0..2, kw=1..2} weight[ic,oc,kh,kw]. W_corner[ic,oc] = sum_{kh=1..2, kw=1..2} weight[ic,oc,kh,kw]. This is a bilinear form: S = X_reduced * W_eff, where X_reduced is some reduced version of the input. Specifically, define for each (b, ic): A_full = sum_{ih=1..H-1, iw=1..W-1} x[b,ic,ih,iw] A_top = sum_{iw=1..W-1} x[b,ic,0,iw] A_left = sum_{ih=1..H-1} x[b,ic,ih,0] A_corner = x[b,ic,0,0] Then S[b,oc] = sum_{ic} [ A_corner * W_corner + A_top * W_top + A_left * W_left + A_full * W_full ] + bias[oc] * H_out * W_out. Then the final output is y[b,oc,1,1] = multiplier * (S[b,oc] / (H_out * W_out) + bias[oc])? Wait, careful: The conv output includes bias added per spatial location. Then we sum over spatial locations. So sum_{oh,ow} out = conv_sum + bias * H_out * W_out. Mean = (conv_sum + bias * H_out * W_out) / (H_out * W_out) = conv_sum/(H_out*W_out) + bias. Then multiply by multiplier: y = multiplier * (conv_sum/(H_out*W_out) + bias). So we don't need to multiply bias by H_out*W_out and then divide; bias just stays as is. Thus y[b,oc] = multiplier * ( S[b,oc] / (H_out * W_out) + bias[oc] ). Where S[b,oc] = sum_{ic} [ A_corner * W_corner + A_top * W_top + A_left * W_left + A_full * W_full ]. So the problem reduces to: 1. Compute 4 partial sums of the input for each (b,ic): corner, top edge (excluding corner), left edge (excluding corner), interior. 2. Compute a dot product with 4 precomputed weight sums per (ic,oc). 3. Add bias, divide by spatial size, multiply by scalar. Step 1 is reading the input (16*64*128*128 ≈ 16.7M floats, ~64 MB). That's trivial. Step 2 is a GEMV-like operation: for each (b,oc), dot product over ic of length 64. With B=16, C_out=128, C_in=64. That's 16*128*64*4 ≈ 524k multiplies. Very small. Step 3 is trivial. So overall, this fused approach is extremely fast: just one pass over the input to compute partial sums, then a tiny matrix multiplication. But is this mathematically correct for ConvTranspose2d? Let's double-check the output size and mapping. PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d with stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1. The exact formula for output size in PyTorch is: H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 With dilation=1, kernel_size=3: H_out = (H_in - 1)*2 - 2*1 + (3-1) + 1 + 1 = (H_in-1)*2 - 2 + 2 + 1 + 1 = (H_in-1)*2 + 2 = 2*H_in. Wait, that's different from earlier because I missed the +1 in the formula. Let's recompute carefully. PyTorch docs for ConvTranspose2d: `H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1` Plug in H_in=128, stride=2, padding=1, kernel_size=3, dilation=1, output_padding=1: H_out = (127)*2 - 2*1 + 1*(2) + 1 + 1 = 254 - 2 + 2 + 1 + 1 = 256. Yes, same result. Good. Now, the mapping. In PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d, the operation is equivalent to computing the convolution of a zero-inserted input with the weight, but with padding and output padding applied to the output. The effective receptive field for each input pixel is defined by the transposed convolution. The actual forward method of ConvTranspose2d uses `conv_transpose2d` which under the hood computes the gradient of a conv2d. It is equivalent to a direct convolution between the input (upsampled) and the weight, but there are subtle boundary issues. Let's test the boundary effect by considering the simplest case: H_in=1, W_in=1, stride=2, padding=1, kernel_size=3, output_padding=1. Then H_out = (1-1)*2 -2 + 2 + 1 + 1 = 2. So output is 2x2. For input pixel at (0,0), the output positions are determined by the formula. In transposed conv, the weight is applied centered at positions that correspond to the input. Actually, one way to think: conv_transpose2d(input, weight, stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1) is equivalent to conv2d of input with weight rotated 180 degrees (if we consider transposed conv as gradient of conv). But since we just care about the sum over output, maybe there's a more robust way. Let's derive from the definition of transposed convolution as the adjoint of convolution. Consider a direct convolution Conv2d(in_channels=C_out, out_channels=C_in, kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1). Wait, the adjoint of conv2d with stride 2 is conv_transpose2d with stride 2. The direct conv maps from [B, C_out, H_out, W_out] to [B, C_in, H_in, W_in]. The adjoint maps back. Specifically, let y = Conv2d(x, W, stride=2, padding=1) where x is [B, C_out, H_out, W_out] and y is [B, C_in, H_in, W_in]. Then the transpose operation z = ConvTranspose2d(y, W, stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1) satisfies: <z, x> = <y, Conv2d(x, W)> (up to output_padding details). So for each z[b,oc,oh,ow], it contributes to y[b,ic,ih,iw] via W[ic,oc,kh,kw] where ih = floor((oh+padding - kh)/stride) etc. But for our purpose, we want sum_{oh,ow} z[b,oc,oh,ow] * 1 = <z, 1>. This is the inner product with an all-ones tensor of shape [B, C_out, H_out, W_out]. By adjoint property, <z, 1> = <y, Conv2d(1, W)>? Wait, we need to be careful. If we define the linear operator T: R^{B*C_out*H_out*W_out} -> R^{B*C_in*H_in*W_in} as T(x) = Conv2d(x, W, stride=2, padding=1). Then ConvTranspose2d(y, W, stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1) is approximately the adjoint T^*(y). But note that T^* maps from R^{B*C_in*H_in*W_in} to R^{B*C_out*H_out*W_out}. For any vector u in the output space of T^* (i.e., shape of z), and v in the input space of T (i.e., shape of y), we have: < T^*(v), u > = < v, T(u) >. If we set u = all-ones tensor of shape [B, C_out, H_out, W_out], then: sum_{oh,ow} T^*(v)[b,oc,oh,ow] = < T^*(v), 1 > = < v, T(1) >. So the sum over output of transposed conv of v is equal to the inner product of v with the direct convolution of the all-ones tensor. This is a very powerful identity! It avoids worrying about boundaries of transposed conv; we just need to compute the direct convolution of an all-ones input with the same weight but as a standard conv (with matching stride, padding). Wait, we need to match the exact parameters. The direct conv that corresponds to the transpose has: - Input channels = C_out - Output channels = C_in - kernel_size = k - stride = stride - padding = padding - dilation = 1 And weight shape for direct conv is [C_in, C_out, kH, kW] (same as transposed conv weight in PyTorch). Yes, PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d weight is (in_channels, out_channels, kH, kW) = (C_in, C_out, kH, kW). The direct conv with this weight mapping from C_out to C_in is indeed Conv2d(C_out, C_in, k, stride=stride, padding=padding). So T(1) = Conv2d(1_{[B,C_out,H_out,W_out]}, weight, stride=2, padding=1). This yields a tensor of shape [B, C_in, H_in, W_in]. Then <v, T(1)> = sum_{b,ic,ih,iw} v[b,ic,ih,iw] * T(1)[b,ic,ih,iw]. So the sum over (oh,ow) of ConvTranspose2d(v) is simply the elementwise product of v and T(1), summed over all dims. This is amazing! It means we don't need to compute the transposed conv at all to get the spatial sum. We just need to compute the direct conv of an all-ones tensor. But computing direct conv of all-ones is even easier: it's just summing the weights over the output channel dimension, applied with stride and padding. Specifically, for each (b, ic, ih, iw): T(1)[b,ic,ih,iw] = sum_{oc=0}^{C_out-1} sum_{kh,kw where (ih,iw) is in receptive field of (oh,ow)} weight[ic, oc, kh, kw] * 1 + bias? Wait, direct conv of 1 has no bias because it's a direct conv; the bias is part of the transposed conv. In our original problem, the transposed conv includes bias. The sum over output of bias is bias[oc] * H_out * W_out. So S[b,oc] = sum_{ic,ih,iw} v[b,ic,ih,iw] * T(1)[b,ic,ih,iw] + bias[oc] * H_out * W_out. Where v is the input to the transposed conv (our x). Now T(1) is independent of b (since input is all ones). So T(1)[ic,ih,iw] is same for all b. It's the result of convolving an all-ones input of shape [C_out, H_out, W_out] with weight [C_in, C_out, 3, 3], stride=2, padding=1. But wait: H_out and W_out are the spatial dimensions of the output of the transposed conv, which are inputs to the direct conv. For the direct conv, the input spatial size is H_out=256. The output spatial size is H_in=128. So T(1) has shape [B, C_in, 128, 128]. Let's compute T(1). Input to direct conv is all ones, shape [C_out, 256, 256]. Weight is [C_in, C_out, 3, 3]. Stride=2, padding=1. For each output position (ih, iw) in T(1), it's the dot product of the weight with a patch of the all-ones input. Since the input is all ones, T(1)[ic, ih, iw] = sum_{oc=0}^{C_out-1} sum_{kh=0}^{2} sum_{kw=0}^{2} weight[ic, oc, kh, kw] * 1_{valid}. The valid mask depends on the boundaries of the direct conv input (size 256). With padding=1, stride=2, output size = floor((256 + 2*1 - 3)/2) + 1 = floor(255/2)+1 = 127+1 = 128. For output ih=0, the input patch is centered at oh = -1 + 0*2? Actually the input indices are oh = ih*stride - padding + kh = 0*2 -1 + kh = -1+kh. So for kh=0, oh=-1 (invalid). So the number of valid positions per patch varies at the borders. But we can compute T(1) offline in Python (or in the kernel) by running a standard conv2d of ones and reading the result. However, the most efficient approach is to precompute the 4 sums of weight similarly to before, but now it's over oc. Wait, but we can simplify T(1) computation. Since the direct conv input is all ones, T(1)[ic, ih, iw] is simply the sum of weight[ic, oc, kh, kw] over all oc and over all valid (kh,kw) for that (ih,iw). Since padding=1, the invalid positions are only at the top and left edges of the output. Specifically: - For ih=0, iw=0: valid kh in {1,2}, kw in {1,2}. - For ih=0, iw>0: valid kh in {1,2}, kw in {0,1,2} (except maybe iw near right edge?). Let's check right edge. Max output iw=127. The input oh for ih=127 is 127*2 -1 + kh = 253+kh. kh in {0,1,2} => oh in {253,254,255}. All < 256. So right edge is fully valid. Bottom edge similarly fully valid because H_out=256. So only top and left edges have missing kernel positions. Thus T(1) can be expressed using 4 weight sums per ic: W_sum_full[ic] = sum_{oc, kh=0..2, kw=0..2} weight[ic, oc, kh, kw]. W_sum_top[ic] = sum_{oc, kh=1..2, kw=0..2} weight[ic, oc, kh, kw]. W_sum_left[ic] = sum_{oc, kh=0..2, kw=1..2} weight[ic, oc, kh, kw]. W_sum_corner[ic] = sum_{oc, kh=1..2, kw=1..2} weight[ic, oc, kh, kw]. Then: T(1)[ic, 0, 0] = W_sum_corner[ic]. T(1)[ic, 0, iw>0] = W_sum_top[ic]. T(1)[ic, ih>0, 0] = W_sum_left[ic]. T(1)[ic, ih>0, iw>0] = W_sum_full[ic]. Thus S[b,oc] = sum_{ic,ih,iw} x[b,ic,ih,iw] * T(1)[ic,ih,iw]? Wait, the adjoint identity gave: sum_{oh,ow} ConvTranspose2d(x)[b,oc,oh,ow] = sum_{ic,ih,iw} x[b,ic,ih,iw] * T(1)[ic,ih,iw]? Let's check the dimensions. T(1) has shape [C_in, H_in, W_in]. The inner product is over ic, ih, iw. But then the result has no oc dependence? That can't be right. Let's re-derive carefully. Define direct convolution operator A: input u (shape [B, C_out, H_out, W_out]) -> output y (shape [B, C_in, H_in, W_in]): y[b, ic, ih, iw] = sum_{oc, kh, kw} u[b, oc, oh, ow] * weight[ic, oc, kh, kw] + bias? Usually direct conv has bias, but let's ignore bias for the linear operator part. The transposed conv operator A^T: input x (shape [B, C_in, H_in, W_in]) -> output z (shape [B, C_out, H_out, W_out]): z = A^T(x). By definition of adjoint: For all u, x: sum_{b,oc,oh,ow} A^T(x)[b,oc,oh,ow] * u[b,oc,oh,ow] = sum_{b,ic,ih,iw} x[b,ic,ih,iw] * A(u)[b,ic,ih,iw]. If we choose u such that u[b, oc, oh, ow] = 1 if oc=oc0, else 0. Then LHS = sum_{oh,ow} z[b, oc0, oh, ow]. RHS = sum_{b,ic,ih,iw} x[b,ic,ih,iw] * A(u)[b,ic,ih,iw]. But A(u) depends on oc0. So: sum_{oh,ow} z[b,oc0,oh,ow] = sum_{ic,ih,iw} x[b,ic,ih,iw] * A(u_{oc0})[ic,ih,iw]. Where u_{oc0} is a tensor that is 1 for channel oc0 and 0 elsewhere, shape [B, C_out, H_out, W_out]. Thus: sum_{oh,ow} z[b,oc0,oh,ow] = sum_{ic,ih,iw} x[b,ic,ih,iw] * (sum_{kh,kw valid} weight[ic, oc0, kh, kw]). This matches our earlier direct derivation! And it shows that the sum depends on oc0. So the adjoint method didn't simplify much beyond confirming the direct derivation. But it did show that for each oc, the weight sum is over kh,kw valid. And because the input to direct conv is all ones for a single channel, the boundary conditions are determined by the direct conv's padding, stride, and input size. So the earlier 4-sum approach is correct. Let's verify the boundary conditions again for the sum over kh,kw valid. We had: sum_{oh,ow} z[b,oc,oh,ow] = sum_{ic,ih,iw} x[b,ic,ih,iw] * W_{oc, valid}(ih,iw). Where W_{oc, valid}(ih,iw) = sum_{kh=0..2, kw=0..2, such that oh = ih*stride - padding + kh is in [0, H_out-1] and similarly for ow} weight[ic, oc, kh, kw]. Given stride=2, padding=1, H_out=256. For a given ih, the valid kh are those where 0 <= ih*2 - 1 + kh <= 255. => 1 - ih*2 <= kh <= 256 - ih*2. Since kh in [0,2]: kh >= max(0, 1 - 2*ih) kh <= min(2, 255 - 2*ih) ??? Wait, 256 - ih*2 means kh <= 256 - 2*ih. For ih=0: kh >= max(0,1) = 1; kh <= min(2,256) = 2. So kh in {1,2}. (2 values) For ih=1: kh >= max(0, -1) = 0; kh <= min(2, 254) = 2. So kh in {0,1,2}. (3 values) ... For ih=127: kh >= max(0, 1-254) = 0; kh <= min(2, 256-254=2) = 2. So kh in {0,1,2}. (3 values) So for ih >= 1, all 3 kh are valid. Similarly for iw >= 1, all 3 kw are valid. Thus the four cases are exactly: - (ih=0, iw=0): kh∈{1,2}, kw∈{1,2} - (ih=0, iw>0): kh∈{1,2}, kw∈{0,1,2} - (ih>0, iw=0): kh∈{0,1,2}, kw∈{1,2} - (ih>0, iw>0): kh∈{0,1,2}, kw∈{0,1,2} Therefore the earlier decomposition is correct. Now, we can compute the four weight sums in Python (or in CUDA at init) and store them as parameters. Since `ModelNew` will have `self.conv_transpose` as the same layer, we can compute these sums in `forward` (or better, at init) from `self.conv_transpose.weight`. But the evaluation compares model outputs; so as long as we read the same weight, it's fine. Given that H_in=W_in=128, the input is moderate. The main cost of a direct kernel would be reading the input once. Let's design the kernel: **Kernel approach 1: Precompute weight sums on CPU, then just read input and compute dot product.** In `__init__`, we compute: - `self.register_buffer('w_corner', torch.empty(C_in, C_out))` - `self.register_buffer('w_top', torch.empty(C_in, C_out))` - `self.register_buffer('w_left', torch.empty(C_in, C_out))` - `self.register_buffer('w_full', torch.empty(C_in, C_out))` We fill them by summing the appropriate slices of `self.conv_transpose.weight`. Then in `forward`, we call a CUDA kernel that takes x and these buffers, and computes: - partial sums per (b, ic): corner, top, left, interior. - Then for each (b, oc), compute: out = multiplier * ( (sum_{ic} (p_corner[ic]*w_corner[ic,oc] + p_top[ic]*w_top[ic,oc] + p_left[ic]*w_left[ic,oc] + p_interior[ic]*w_full[ic,oc])) / (H_out*W_out) + bias[oc] ) We can do this in two phases: - Phase 1: Compute partial sums of x, store in a tensor [B, C_in, 4] (or 4 separate tensors). - Phase 2: Compute the final output using GEMM or custom kernel. Phase 1 can be a custom CUDA kernel that reads x once and accumulates per (b,ic) the 4 sums. Phase 2 can be done with a simple CUDA kernel or even PyTorch operations (e.g., `torch.matmul`). Given that Phase 2 is extremely small, using PyTorch matmul is fine and very efficient. Phase 1 is the main custom kernel. Alternatively, we can do everything in one custom kernel: each thread block handles a (b, ic) and computes the 4 sums, stores in shared memory, then does the dot product with the weight sums for all oc? But that would require each thread block to read weight [C_in, C_out] which is 64*128=8192 floats, small enough for shared memory. But the number of blocks would be B*C_in = 1024. Each block would have to compute 4 partial sums (reading 128*128 floats from x) and then compute 128 output values. That could be efficient. However, using two kernels is simpler and likely sufficient: Phase 1 reduces [16,64,128,128] to [16,64,4]. Then Phase 2 is a batched matrix multiply [16,1,64] @ [16,64,128]? Actually we can reshape. Let's think about Phase 1 using PyTorch operations: can we compute the 4 partial sums efficiently without a custom kernel? Yes: - `x_interior = x[..., 1:, 1:].sum(dim=[2,3])` - `x_top = x[..., 0, 1:].sum(dim=2)` - `x_left = x[..., 1:, 0].sum(dim=2)` - `x_corner = x[..., 0, 0]` Then stack them. This is 4 reduction ops in PyTorch, each reading x (or slices). But it reads x multiple times from memory. Since x is 64 MB, reading it 4 times is 256 MB, which is fine but maybe bandwidth-limited. A custom kernel reading x once (64 MB) and computing all 4 sums would be faster. But wait, PyTorch might be able to compute these reductions quite efficiently. However, since the final output is tiny, the fastest path is just one custom kernel that reads x, computes partial sums, and then immediately does the weighted sum to produce the final output. That avoids intermediate storage and extra kernel launches. **Single fused kernel design:** Grid: B blocks per batch? Actually we can parallelize over (b, oc). There are B*C_out = 16*128 = 2048 output elements. For each output element (b, oc), we need to compute: out[b, oc] = multiplier * ( (sum_{ic} ( w_corner[ic,oc] * x_corner + w_top[ic,oc] * x_top + w_left[ic,oc] * x_left + w_full[ic,oc] * x_interior )) / (H_out*W_out) + bias[oc] ) Where x_corner = x[b, ic, 0, 0], etc. So each thread/block computes this sum over ic. If we use one thread per (b, oc), then each thread loops over ic=0..63, and for each ic, it needs to compute 4 terms. But the x values depend only on (b, ic), not on oc. So if we have one thread per (b, oc), each thread would read the same x values for the same (b, ic) but different weights. This means x is read C_out times (128 times) from global memory. That is bad. Better to have blocks or threads parallelize over (b, ic), computing partial sums of x that are reused across all oc. Then reduce across ic. Option A: Two-phase kernel as above. Option B: One kernel with shared memory. Each block handles one (b). Within block, threads parallelize over ic (64) or spatial (128*128). Let's think. Since ic=64 is small, we could assign each warp to one ic. For a given b, we have 64 input channels. We can have a block with 64 warps? That's 2048 threads, too many (max 1024). Let's use 64 threads (one per ic). Each thread reads its channel's x values, computes the 4 partial sums, and stores them in shared memory [64][4]. Then after __syncthreads(), we need to compute for each oc (128) the dot product over ic. We can have the 64 threads cooperate to compute the 128 outputs. For example, each thread computes a partial sum for a subset of oc. But the weights are [64,128]. We can load weight columns in a tiled manner. Alternatively, after computing the partial sums in shared memory, we can just call `__syncthreads()` and have each thread (or a subset) compute the final output for a few oc. Since 64 threads * 128 oc = 8192 dot products, each dot product is length 64. If each thread computes 2 oc, that's 128 dot products per thread, each involves 64 multiplies and adds. That's ~8192 FLOPs per thread, trivial. The main cost is reading the weight matrix: 64*128*4 = 32KB per block. Shared memory can hold that. Actually we can store w_corner, w_top, w_left, w_full in shared memory as [4][64][128]? That's 4*64*128*4 = 131KB. That may exceed shared memory (usually 48KB or 96KB). We could store just one of them at a time or use registers. But we can do even simpler: Since the compute after partial sums is tiny, we can just do it on the CPU using PyTorch after the partial sums kernel. The partial sums kernel is the heavy memory part. Phase 2 will be a tiny matrix multiplication that will be instantaneous. So the plan: 1. Custom kernel: given x, compute partial sums per (b, ic) -> tensor p of shape [B, C_in, 4]. 2. PyTorch: compute linear combination with weight sums and bias. We can also use a custom kernel for partial sums and then a PyTorch matmul. That's likely fast enough. But maybe we can fuse the matmul into the kernel to reduce overhead. However, kernel launch overhead is small; 2 launches is fine. Let's design the partial sums kernel. We can create a kernel where each thread block handles one (b, ic), and each thread loops over the spatial pixels of that channel, accumulating into 4 registers. But there are B*C_in = 1024 blocks. Actually, simpler approach: use `grid-strided loop` with each thread processing some pixels. Each block handles one (b, ic). Threads read elements and accumulate into 4 shared memory accumulators? Wait, we need 4 separate sums: corner, top, left, interior. We can have each thread accumulate 4 local registers, then do block-level reduction (warp shuffle or shared mem) to sum across threads. But we can be even more clever: treat the spatial domain as 1D array of size H*W = 16384. We launch blocks of size 256. Each block handles one (b, ic). Number of blocks = B*C_in = 1024. Each thread loops with stride blockDim.x over the 16384 elements. For each element at linear index idx, compute ih = idx / W, iw = idx % W. Then depending on (ih, iw), add to the appropriate accumulator. After the loop, do a warp shuffle reduction or shared memory reduction for the 4 accumulators. Then thread 0 writes the 4 values to global memory. This is very straightforward and efficient. Each block reads 16384 floats = 64KB. At 2TB/s bandwidth, that's 32us per block. But we have 1024 blocks, and they run in parallel on SMs (maybe 80 SMs). So total time is on the order of 1024/80 * 32us ≈ 400us? Wait, that's not right because memory is shared. Throughput is limited by memory bandwidth. Total memory read is B*C_in*H*W*4 bytes = 16*64*128*128*4 = 67,108,864 bytes ≈ 64 MB. At 1.5 TB/s, that's ~43 microseconds. So the kernel should take around 50-100 microseconds. That's very fast. Then Phase 2 is a tiny matmul: [B, C_in] @ [C_in, C_out] with 4 terms. Actually it's: result = (p_corner @ w_corner + p_top @ w_top + p_left @ w_left + p_full @ w_full) / (H_out*W_out) + bias Then multiply by multiplier. In PyTorch, we can compute: ``` s = torch.zeros(B, C_out, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) s += p[..., 0] @ w_corner # p shape [B, C_in, 4] s += p[..., 1] @ w_top s += p[..., 2] @ w_left s += p[..., 3] @ w_full out = (s / (H_out * W_out) + bias.view(1, -1)) * multiplier ``` Each matmul is [16,64] @ [64,128] = very small (16*64*128*2 = 262k FLOPs). Four of them is ~1M FLOPs. On GPU, this is microseconds. So total time should be dominated by the partial sum kernel (~50-100us). The reference likely takes a few milliseconds (conv transpose on 512MB output is heavy). So we can get a huge speedup. But we should verify correctness thoroughly. Let's implement the kernel. **CUDA Kernel: partial_sums** Input: x [B, C_in, H, W] Output: p [B, C_in, 4] Launch: dim3 grid(B * C_in); dim3 block(256); // or 128 Each block corresponds to one (b, ic). In the kernel: ``` __global__ void partial_sums_kernel(const float* x, float* p, int B, int C, int H, int W) { int b = blockIdx.x / C; int c = blockIdx.x % C; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nthreads = blockDim.x; const float* x_ptr = x + ((b * C + c) * H * W); float sum_corner = 0.0f; float sum_top = 0.0f; float sum_left = 0.0f; float sum_full = 0.0f; int total = H * W; for (int idx = tid; idx < total; idx += nthreads) { int h = idx / W; int w = idx % W; float val = x_ptr[idx]; if (h == 0 && w == 0) { sum_corner += val; } else if (h == 0) { sum_top += val; } else if (w == 0) { sum_left += val; } else { sum_full += val; } } // block reduction for 4 sums using shared memory __shared__ float s_corner[256]; __shared__ float s_top[256]; __shared__ float s_left[256]; __shared__ float s_full[256]; s_corner[tid] = sum_corner; s_top[tid] = sum_top; s_left[tid] = sum_left; s_full[tid] = sum_full; __syncthreads(); // reduce for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { s_corner[tid] += s_corner[tid + s]; s_top[tid] += s_top[tid + s]; s_left[tid] += s_left[tid + s]; s_full[tid] += s_full[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid == 0) { int p_idx = (b * C + c) * 4; p[p_idx + 0] = s_corner[0]; p[p_idx + 1] = s_top[0]; p[p_idx + 2] = s_left[0]; p[p_idx + 3] = s_full[0]; } } ``` This is simple and should be correct. However, we must consider fp32 precision. The reductions sum up to ~16384 elements. The sum magnitude could be up to ~16384. Float32 has ~7 decimal digits, so summing in arbitrary order could cause some rounding error, but since the reference uses mean over 256*256=65536 elements (first mean) and then mean over 1 (second mean), the reference itself accumulates in a slightly different order (via cuDNN conv and then reduction). The tolerance is atol=rtol=1e-4. Summation order differences for 16k elements should be well within that. But we can improve accuracy by using warp-shuffle reduction or double buffering. But it's probably fine. Wait, the reference does conv_transpose (which computes each output pixel as a sum of up to 576 products, then adds bias, then computes mean over 65536). Our kernel computes the sum directly as dot product of partial sums and weight sums. The partial sums reduction uses a different order. The error due to ordering is acceptable. Potential issue: PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d may use different implementation (e.g., im2col + gemm) which may have slightly different rounding. Our exact arithmetic matches the mathematical identity, so if we compute partial sums accurately enough, we should match within tolerance. One thing: We must include bias correctly. Wait, does PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d add bias before or after the conv? It adds bias per output channel to every spatial location. So the mean includes bias. In our formula: mean = (spatial_sum_of_conv + bias * H_out * W_out) / (H_out * W_out) = spatial_sum_of_conv / (H_out * W_out) + bias. So bias is just added after dividing the conv spatial sum by H_out*W_out. But wait: The reference does `x = self.conv_transpose(x)`, then `x = x * multiplier`, then `x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2,3], keepdim=True)` twice. The first mean computes the average over H_out*W_out. The second mean over [2,3] of a [B,C,1,1] tensor just returns the same tensor. So the output is: `multiplier * mean(conv_out, dim=(2,3), keepdim=True)`. Since mean includes adding bias to each spatial element and averaging, the bias contribution is exactly bias. So our formula is correct. Potential edge case: output_padding. Does output_padding affect the sum? Output padding adds extra rows/cols of zeros to the output (or does it affect the positions?). Actually, output_padding in ConvTranspose2d increases the output size by adding extra rows/columns to the output, but these are part of the valid output. Does the conv_transpose kernel place values there, or are they zeros? In PyTorch, output_padding adjusts the output shape by effectively adding extra stride at the end. It doesn't insert zeros; it shifts the convolution to produce a larger output. The values in the extra rows/cols are computed by the convolution just like any other output pixel. So our derivation using output size H_out=256 already accounts for output_padding because H_out includes it. The mapping boundaries we used are based on H_out=256. And indeed, for the bottom/right edges, all kernel positions are valid, which is because output_padding ensures the "last" input pixel maps to near the bottom/right edge, covering the extra area. So the sum formula holds. Let's double-check with a small example in Python to be absolutely sure. We'll write a quick test script. But we can't run Python code here directly, but we can reason. Let's take a tiny case: B=1, C_in=1, C_out=1, H_in=2, W_in=2, kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1. H_out = (2-1)*2 - 2 + 3 + 1 = 2 - 2 + 4 = 4. Output 4x4. Weight w[0,0] = [[a,b,c],[d,e,f],[g,h,i]]. Input x = [[p,q],[r,s]]. ConvTranspose2d output at each spatial location (oh,ow): We can compute by oversampling and convolving, or using the adjoint. Let's compute using the direct definition: For each input pixel (ih,iw), it contributes to output region: oh = ih*stride - padding + kh = ih*2 - 1 + kh ow = iw*2 - 1 + kw ih=0, iw=0: oh=-1+kh, ow=-1+kw. kh=0 -> oh=-1 invalid kh=1 -> oh=0 valid kh=2 -> oh=1 valid Similarly for kw. So contributes to output positions: (0,0): w[1,1]*p = e*p (0,1): w[1,2]*p = f*p (1,0): w[2,1]*p = h*p (1,1): w[2,2]*p = i*p ih=0, iw=1: oh=-1+kh, ow=2-1+kw = 1+kw. kh valid: 1,2 -> oh=0,1. kw valid: 0,1,2 -> ow=1,2,3. So contributes to: (0,1): w[1,0]*q = d*q? Wait, weight indexing: weight[ic,oc,kh,kw]. So for kh=1,kw=0 -> w[1,0] = d? Assuming 3x3 matrix: [[a,b,c], [d,e,f], [g,h,i]] kh=1,kw=0 is d. (0,1): d*q (0,2): e*q (0,3): f*q (1,1): g*q? kh=2,kw=0 -> g. (1,2): h*q (1,3): i*q Wait, kh=2,kw=0 gives w[2,0] = g. ih=1, iw=0: oh=2-1+kh = 1+kh. ow=-1+kw. kh valid: 0,1,2 -> oh=1,2,3. kw valid: 1,2 -> ow=0,1. So: (1,0): w[0,1]*r = b*r? Wait, kh=0,kw=1 -> w[0,1] = b. (1,1): c*r (kh=0,kw=2) (2,0): e*r? kh=1,kw=1 -> e. (2,1): f*r (3,0): h*r? kh=2,kw=1 -> h. (3,1): i*r ih=1, iw=1: oh=1+kh, ow=1+kw. kh=0,1,2 -> oh=1,2,3. kw=0,1,2 -> ow=1,2,3. So all 9 positions valid. (1,1): a*s (1,2): b*s (1,3): c*s (2,1): d*s (2,2): e*s (2,3): f*s (3,1): g*s (3,2): h*s (3,3): i*s Now let's compute the sum over all oh,ow. Let's sum each output position contributions: (0,0): e*p (0,1): f*p + d*q (0,2): e*q (0,3): f*q (1,0): h*p + b*r (1,1): i*p + g*q + c*r + a*s (1,2): h*q + b*s (1,3): i*q + c*s (2,0): e*r (2,1): f*r + d*s (2,2): e*s (2,3): f*s (3,0): h*r (3,1): i*r + g*s (3,2): h*s (3,3): i*s Total sum = p*(e+f+h+i) + q*(d+e+f+g+h+i) + r*(b+c+e+f+h+i) + s*(a+b+c+d+e+f+g+h+i) Group by input pixel: - For p (ih=0, iw=0): sum of weights where kh∈{1,2}, kw∈{1,2} = e+f+h+i. This matches W_corner. - For q (ih=0, iw=1): sum of weights where kh∈{1,2}, kw∈{0,1,2} = d+e+f+g+h+i. This matches W_top? Wait, q is top edge (ih=0, iw>0). For ih=0, the valid kh are {1,2} (since oh=-1+kh, need oh>=0 => kh>=1). Valid kw are {0,1,2} (since ow=1+kw, need ow<4 => kw<3 => kw<=2, and kw>=0 always). So sum is d+e+f+g+h+i. That's exactly W_top. - For r (ih=1, iw=0): valid kw are {1,2} (ow=-1+kw => kw>=1), valid kh are {0,1,2} (oh=1+kh, always <4 since max=3). Sum = b+c+e+f+h+i. That's W_left. - For s (ih=1, iw=1): all valid. Sum = a+b+c+d+e+f+g+h+i. That's W_full. This perfectly matches our 4 cases. Good. So the math is solid. Now implementing `ModelNew`. At init, we need to: 1. Call `super().__init__()` 2. Create `self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(...)` to match reference parameter structure. 3. Compute the 4 weight sum tensors from `self.conv_transpose.weight`. We may want to move them to the GPU when needed. But to keep it simple, we can compute them inside `forward` on the fly using PyTorch operations. That would involve slicing the weight and summing. The weight is small (64*128*3*3=73,728 floats). Doing 4 sums on CPU or GPU is negligible. However, we want `forward` to be fast. Let's precompute them as buffers. We need to be careful: `self.conv_transpose` is created with the given arguments. PyTorch's default initialization is random. The evaluation code may copy state_dict from reference to `ModelNew`. So we must ensure the weight shapes match. They will because we create the same layer. So in `__init__`: ``` class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, multiplier): super().__init__() self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding) self.multiplier = multiplier # Precompute weight sums with torch.no_grad(): w = self.conv_transpose.weight # [in_ch, out_ch, kH, kW] # using float32 self.register_buffer('w_full', w.sum(dim=[2,3])) # [in_ch, out_ch] self.register_buffer('w_top', w[:,:,1:,:].sum(dim=[2,3])) # [in_ch, out_ch] self.register_buffer('w_left', w[:,:,:,1:].sum(dim=[2,3])) # [in_ch, out_ch] self.register_buffer('w_corner', w[:,:,1:,1:].sum(dim=[2,3])) # [in_ch, out_ch] ``` Wait, does `w[:,:,1:,:].sum(dim=[2,3])` correspond to top edge? Let's verify indexing. Weight shape: [C_in, C_out, kH, kW]. - `w[:,:,1:,1:]` excludes kh=0 and kw=0 -> valid for (ih=0, iw=0) where kh>=1, kw>=1. Yes, corner. - `w[:,:,1:,:]` excludes kh=0 -> valid for (ih=0, iw>0) where kh>=1, all kw. Yes, top. - `w[:,:,:,1:]` excludes kw=0 -> valid for (ih>0, iw=0) where all kh, kw>=1. Yes, left. - `w[:,:,:,:]` full -> valid for interior. So that's correct. We also need bias: `self.conv_transpose.bias` shape [C_out]. Now the `forward` function: ``` def forward(self, x): B, C_in, H, W = x.shape C_out = self.conv_transpose.out_channels H_out = (H - 1) * self.conv_transpose.stride[0] - 2 * self.conv_transpose.padding[0] + self.conv_transpose.kernel_size[0] + self.conv_transpose.output_padding[0] W_out = ... similarly p = torch.empty(B, C_in, 4, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) # launch kernel to fill p partial_sums(x, p) # compute weighted sums # p: [B, C_in, 4] # w_*: [C_in, C_out] out = torch.zeros(B, C_out, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) out += p[:, :, 0] @ self.w_corner out += p[:, :, 1] @ self.w_top out += p[:, :, 2] @ self.w_left out += p[:, :, 3] @ self.w_full out = out / (H_out * W_out) if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None: out = out + self.conv_transpose.bias.view(1, -1) out = out * self.multiplier return out.view(B, C_out, 1, 1) ``` Wait, the output shape must be [B, C_out, 1, 1]. The reference returns a 4D tensor. One nuance: The partial sums kernel needs to know H, W. We can pass them as arguments. Now, about `load_inline`. We need to write a CUDA C++ source with the kernel and a host launcher function. The host launcher will accept `at::Tensor x` and `at::Tensor p`, and call the kernel. Let's write the C++ host function: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void partial_sums_kernel(const float* x, float* p, int B, int C, int H, int W) { int total_blocks = B * C; if (blockIdx.x >= total_blocks) return; int b = blockIdx.x / C; int c = blockIdx.x % C; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nthreads = blockDim.x; const float* x_ptr = x + ((b * C + c) * H * W); float sum_corner = 0.0f; float sum_top = 0.0f; float sum_left = 0.0f; float sum_interior = 0.0f; int total = H * W; for (int idx = tid; idx < total; idx += nthreads) { int h = idx / W; int w = idx % W; float val = x_ptr[idx]; if (h == 0 && w == 0) { sum_corner += val; } else if (h == 0) { sum_top += val; } else if (w == 0) { sum_left += val; } else { sum_interior += val; } } __shared__ float s[4][256]; // assuming blockDim.x <= 256 // Actually dynamic shared memory or just static size. Block size we will set to 256. s[0][tid] = sum_corner; s[1][tid] = sum_top; s[2][tid] = sum_left; s[3][tid] = sum_interior; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s[0][tid] += s[0][tid + stride]; s[1][tid] += s[1][tid + stride]; s[2][tid] += s[2][tid + stride]; s[3][tid] += s[3][tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid == 0) { int p_idx = (b * C + c) * 4; p[p_idx + 0] = s[0][0]; p[p_idx + 1] = s[1][0]; p[p_idx + 2] = s[2][0]; p[p_idx + 3] = s[3][0]; } } void partial_sums_cuda(const at::Tensor& x, at::Tensor& p) { int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); int threads = 256; int blocks = B * C; partial_sums_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), p.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, H, W); } ``` But we need to handle cases where blockDim.x is not exactly 256. We can launch with 256 threads each time. Since H*W = 16384, 256 threads is fine. However, there is a shared memory size issue: `__shared__ float s[4][256];` uses 4*256*4 = 4096 bytes. That's well within limits. We could also make shared memory dynamic, but static is fine if we hardcode block size to 256. Let's hardcode it for simplicity and assert `blockDim.x <= 256`. But we can also write it more flexibly with dynamic shared memory: ```cpp extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* s_corner = smem; float* s_top = smem + blockDim.x; float* s_left = smem + 2*blockDim.x; float* s_interior = smem + 3*blockDim.x; ``` Then launch with `partial_sums_kernel<<<blocks, threads, 4*threads*sizeof(float)>>>(...)`. That is robust. Let's do that. Now, consider using `TORCH_CHECK` on inputs: ensure x is contiguous float32 on CUDA, p is contiguous float32 on CUDA. Also, the output should be on the same device. We can also fuse everything into one kernel to avoid intermediate `p` allocation and matmul overhead. The matmul overhead is tiny, but the memory allocation for `p` (B*C_in*4 floats = 16*64*4*4 = 16KB) is tiny and fast. But if we want to be absolutely minimal, we can fuse. Fused kernel design: - One block per (b, oc). Block size maybe 128 or 256 threads. - Threads parallelize over ic and spatial? Actually each block computes one output element. To avoid reading x multiple times, we can have threads parallelize over ic (64) and do warp reductions over spatial? No, each ic requires reading H*W=16k floats. 64 * 16k = 1M floats for one output. That's too much read per output. Better to have each block handle a batch b, compute partial sums for all ic (shared memory of 64*4 floats), then compute all oc outputs cooperatively. Specifically: - Launch grid: B blocks (one per batch). - Each block has e.g., 256 threads. - Compute partial sums for all C_in channels. Since total elements per batch = C_in * H * W = 64 * 16384 = 1,048,576. With 256 threads, each thread processes 4096 elements. We can assign each thread a linear index and strided loop over all channels. - We allocate shared memory for partial sums: `float s_partial[64][4];` = 64*4*4 = 1KB. Actually that's small. - We can use global memory atomics to accumulate into a shared buffer. Or each thread reads a value, determines which (c, region) it belongs to, and atomically adds to `s_partial[c][region]` in shared memory. Since shared memory atomics are fast and contention is low (4096 elements per thread, 64 channels, but threads are processing different channels/strides), maybe contention is okay. But better: each thread maintains a local array of 4 partial sums for a subset of channels? That's too many registers. Alternatively, we can compute per-channel partial sums using a two-level reduction: each thread handles a portion of spatial for a fixed channel. But mapping threadIdx to (c, spatial) is tricky. Given the simplicity of the two-kernel approach and the negligible overhead, I think two kernels (or one partial sum kernel + PyTorch matmul) is best. Let's keep the partial sum kernel + PyTorch ops. That's easy to verify and very fast. But the PyTorch matmul `@` with fp32 on [16,64]@[64,128] is extremely fast because cuBLAS handles it. Four such matmuls might have launch overhead, but we can combine them. Actually, we can pre-stack the weight sums into a tensor of shape [4, C_in, C_out]. Then do `torch.einsum('bci,io->bco', p, w_stacked).sum(dim=0)`? No. We can reshape p to [B*4, C_in] and w_stacked to [4, C_in, C_out] and do batched matmul? Not directly because we want sum over the 4. Better to do: ``` out = p[:,:,0] @ w_corner + p[:,:,1] @ w_top + p[:,:,2] @ w_left + p[:,:,3] @ w_full ``` Since each term is small, it should be fine. We can also fuse the 4 matmuls into one by creating a weight tensor of shape [C_in*4, C_out] and a p tensor of shape [B, C_in*4], then doing a single matmul: ``` p_flat = p.view(B, C_in * 4) # [B, 256] w_flat = torch.stack([w_corner, w_top, w_left, w_full], dim=1).view(C_in * 4, C_out) # [256, 128] out = p_flat @ w_flat # [B, 128] ``` This is a single matmul of [16, 256] @ [256, 128] = 524k*2 = ~1M FLOPs. One launch. Better. Let's compute w_flat in `__init__`. ``` w = self.conv_transpose.weight # [C_in, C_out, kH, kW] w_corner = w[:,:,1:,1:].sum(dim=(2,3)) w_top = w[:,:,1:,:].sum(dim=(2,3)) w_left = w[:,:,:,1:].sum(dim=(2,3)) w_full = w.sum(dim=(2,3)) self.register_buffer('w_flat', torch.stack([w_corner, w_top, w_left, w_full], dim=1).view(C_in * 4, C_out)) ``` Then in forward: ``` p = partial_sums(x) # [B, C_in, 4] out = p.view(B, -1) @ self.w_flat # [B, C_out] out = out / (H_out * W_out) if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None: out += self.conv_transpose.bias.unsqueeze(0) out *= self.multiplier return out.view(B, C_out, 1, 1) ``` This is very clean. Now, we must compute H_out and W_out in forward. In `__init__`, we can store the stride, padding, etc. Or we can just compute using the conv_transpose layer's attributes. ``` stride = self.conv_transpose.stride[0] padding = self.conv_transpose.padding[0] kernel_size = self.conv_transpose.kernel_size[0] output_padding = self.conv_transpose.output_padding[0] H_out = (H - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding W_out = (W - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding ``` Wait, W_out formula: same but with W. Upsample the input by inserting zeros 2. Apply a normal convolution Or equivalently, it's a dense linear operation. Each output channel at each spatial location is a weighted sum of input values over a receptive field. If we average over all spatial locations of the output, we might be able to compute this more efficiently. But actually, since the stride > 1, the receptive fields overlap in a complex way. Actually, there's a known mathematical identity: the sum of all output pixels of a convolution equals the sum of all input pixels times the sum of all weights (plus bias times output_size). Sum over all y,x of output[y,x] = sum_{c,i,j} weight[c,i,j] * sum_{y,x} input[c, y+i, x+j]. Actually, let me reconsider. The naive cuDNN conv transpose followed by two means should already be fairly well optimized. To beat PyTorch significantly, we need a smarter approach. Let me reconsider the math. For a transposed convolution: If we think of it as a gradient of convolution... or equivalently as: output = conv_transpose2d(input, weight, bias, stride, padding, output_padding) A key insight: for transposed conv with stride=2, each input element gets multiplied by the kernel and placed in the output with stride-2 spacing. Let me write the custom kernel for stats. ```cuda __global__ void compute_stats( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ stats, int B, int C, int H, int W ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nthreads = blockDim.x; int hw = H * W; const float* ptr = input + (b * C + c) * hw; float total = 0.0f; float row0 = 0.0f; float col0 = 0.0f; float corner = 0.0f; // Each thread processes a subset of elements for (int idx = tid; idx < hw; idx += nthreads) { float val = ptr[idx]; total += val; if (idx < W) row0 += val; // first row if (idx % W == 0) col0 += val; // first column if (idx == 0) corner = val; // corner } // Warp reduction total = warp_reduce_sum(total); row0 = warp_reduce_sum(row0); col0 = warp_reduce_sum(col0); // Cross-warp reduction using shared memory __shared__ float s_total[32]; __shared__ float s_row0[32]; __shared__ float s_col0[32]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp = tid / 32; if (lane == 0) { s_total[warp] = total; s_row0[warp] = row0; s_col0[warp] = col0; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { total = (tid < (nthreads + 31) / 32) ? s_total[tid] : 0.0f; row0 = (tid < (nthreads + 31) / 32) ? s_row0[tid] : 0.0f; col0 = (tid < (nthreads + 31) / 32) ? s_col0[tid] : 0.0f; total = warp_reduce_sum(total); row0 = warp_reduce_sum(row0); col0 = warp_reduce_sum(col0); if (tid == 0) { int stats_idx = (b * C + c) * 4; stats[stats_idx + 0] = total; stats[stats_idx + 1] = row0; stats[stats_idx + 2] = col0; stats[stats_idx + 3] = corner; } } } ``` Wait, `corner` is only set by thread 0 (since only thread with idx==0 sees it). Actually, much simpler: - Use shared memory for all 4 values. - Each thread processes elements and accumulates private sums. - Write to shared memory. - Do tree reduction in shared memory. ```cuda __global__ void compute_stats_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ stats, int B, int C, int H, int W ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nthreads = blockDim.x; int hw = H * W; const float* ptr = input + (b * C + c) * hw; float total = 0.0f; float row0 = 0.0f; float col0 = 0.0f; float corner = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < hw; idx += nthreads) { float val = ptr[idx]; total += val; if (idx < W) row0 += val; if (idx % W == 0) col0 += val; if (idx == 0) corner = val; } __shared__ float s_total[256]; __shared__ float s_row0[256]; __shared__ float s_col0[256]; __shared__ float s_corner[256]; s_total[tid] = total; s_row0[tid] = row0; s_col0[tid] = col0; s_corner[tid] = corner; __syncthreads(); for (int s = nthreads / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { s_total[tid] += s_total[tid + s]; s_row0[tid] += s_row0[tid + s]; s_col0[tid] += s_col0[tid + s]; s_corner[tid] += s_corner[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid == 0) { int idx = (b * C + c) * 4; stats[idx + 0] = s_total[0]; stats[idx + 1] = s_row0[0]; stats[idx + 2] = s_col0[0]; stats[idx + 3] = s_corner[0]; } } ``` This is much cleaner. Then in ModelNew: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, ...): ... # Precompute weight stats w = self.conv_transpose.weight # [C_in, C_out, 3, 3] self.register_buffer('W_sum', w.sum(dim=[2,3]).t()) # [C_out, C_in] self.register_buffer('W_top', w[:,:,0,:].sum(dim=2).t()) # [C_out, C_in] self.register_buffer('W_left', w[:,:,:,0].sum(dim=2).t()) # [C_out, C_in] self.register_buffer('w00', w[:,:,0,0].t()) # [C_out, C_in] self.register_buffer('bias', self.conv_transpose.bias.clone()) # [C_out] del self.conv_transpose def forward(self, x): B, C_in, H, W = x.shape assert C_in == self.in_channels stats = compute_stats(x, B, C_in, H, W) # [B, C_in, 4] total = stats[:, :, 0] row0 = stats[:, :, 1] col0 = stats[:, :, 2] corner = stats[:, :, 3] out = torch.matmul(total, self.W_sum) \ - torch.matmul(row0, self.W_top) \ - torch.matmul(col0, self.W_left) \ + torch.matmul(corner, self.w00) out = out / (4.0 * H * W) + self.bias out = out * self.multiplier return out.view(B, self.out_channels, 1, 1) ``` Wait, I need to check the transpose. - w has shape [C_in, C_out, 3, 3] - w.sum(dim=[2,3]) has shape [C_in, C_out] - After `.t()`, it has shape [C_out, C_in] - `total` has shape [B, C_in] - `total @ W_sum` with W_sum=[C_out, C_in] doesn't work. Let me re-verify for 2D: Each input pixel in[b, c_in, h, w] with weight W[c_in, c_out, i, j] contributes to output pixel: h_out = 2*h + i - 1 w_out = 2*w + j - 1 The valid condition is: 0 <= h_out <= H_out - 1 = 2H - 1 0 <= w_out <= W_out - 1 = 2W - 1 For i=0: 0 <= 2h - 1 <= 2H-1 => h in [1, H-1] For i=1: h in [0, H-1] For i=2: h in [0, H-1] Same for j and w. Then mean = multiplier * (sum / (H_out * W_out) + bias) = multiplier * (sum / (4*H*W) + bias) Great, the formula is verified. Now, there is one more subtlety. In the reference, the bias is added AFTER the conv_transpose, and then multiplied by multiplier. So: x = conv_transpose(input) # includes bias added to every output pixel x = x * multiplier x = mean(x) So mean = mean(conv_output * multiplier) = multiplier * mean(conv_output) And mean(conv_output) = mean(conv_without_bias + bias) = mean(conv_without_bias) + bias Because bias is added to every pixel, its mean is just bias. So: final = multiplier * (spatial_mean(conv_without_bias) + bias) And spatial_mean(conv_without_bias) = sum / (H_out * W_out) So: final = multiplier * (sum / (H_out * W_out) + bias) Which is exactly what I have. Now, one more check: the second mean. x = mean(x, dim=[2,3], keepdim=True) If x is [B, C, 1, 1], then mean over dims [2,3] is still [B, C, 1, 1] with the same values. So yes, it's a no-op. I'll proceed with writing the kernel. Wait, I should also consider whether we can compute everything in a single custom kernel. Let me think about this. If I write one kernel that computes stats and then immediately computes the output, I need: 1. Block (b, c_in) computes stats for that channel 2. All blocks synchronize 3. Then blocks compute output Using grid synchronization (cooperative groups) is possible but adds complexity. Since stats are only 16 * 64 * 4 = 4096 floats, writing them to global memory and reading back is negligible. So two kernels or even custom + PyTorch matmul is fine. I'll go with: 1. Custom CUDA kernel for stats 2. PyTorch operations for the rest This keeps the CUDA code simple and leverages PyTorch's optimized matmul. Wait, actually, I can write a single CUDA kernel that computes everything if I structure it as one block per batch element. Let me consider this. One block per batch, with 256 threads: 1. The block loads all C_in channels for that batch. - Total data = 64 * 128 * 128 * 4 bytes = 4 MB. Too big for shared memory. 2. So we can't load everything into shared memory. Instead, the block can compute stats for one channel at a time, accumulate partial results for output, and then finalize. Specifically: - Each block processes batch b. - For c_in = 0 to 63: - 256 threads read the 128x128 channel and compute total, row0, col0, corner. - Store in shared memory (256 floats for each stat, or just 4 floats after reduction). - Each thread (or a subset) reads the weight values for some output channels. - Accumulate partial output sums. - After all c_in processed, finalize output with bias and multiplier. This way, we don't write stats to global memory at all! Block structure: - 256 threads. - For each c_in: - Load 128x128 elements and reduce to 4 stats (~1 KB shared memory after reduction). - Then have each thread compute a subset of output channels using the stats and weights. Number of output channels = 128. With 256 threads, each thread could compute 0.5 channels? Or we use 128 threads for output, 128 for loading... hmm. Actually, we can have 128 threads that compute output. The other 128 threads can idle during the matmul phase. Or better: after computing stats, we broadcast them to all threads. Then each thread computes 128/256 = 0.5 output channels... not integer. With 128 threads: each computes exactly 1 output channel. But then loading 128x128 with 128 threads means each reads 128 elements. Also fine. Let's do 128 threads per block, one block per batch. For batch b: - shared float s_stats[4]; // after reduction - For c_in in 0..63: - Threads load channel c_in and compute stats - Tree reduction to s_stats[0..3] - __syncthreads() - Thread c_out (0..127) computes: partial[c_out] += s_stats[0] * W_sum[c_in, c_out] - s_stats[1] * W_top[c_in, c_out] - s_stats[2] * W_left[c_in, c_out] + s_stats[3] * w00[c_in, c_out] - __syncthreads() - After loop: thread c_out writes: out[b, c_out] = (partial[c_out] / (4*H*W) + bias[c_out]) * multiplier But partial needs to be stored in shared memory or registers. 128 floats per thread block = 512 bytes in shared memory. Very manageable. Actually, each thread can keep one float `acc` in a register. Thread c_out accumulates for output channel c_out. No shared memory needed for partial sums. But threads need to read weights. Each thread c_out reads W_sum[c_in, c_out], W_top[c_in, c_out], W_left[c_in, c_out], w00[c_in, c_out] for each c_in. Weight memory access pattern: - W_sum is [C_in, C_out] = [64, 128]. - Thread c_out reads column c_out. So thread 0 reads W_sum[0,0], W_sum[1,0], ..., W_sum[63,0]. - Thread 1 reads W_sum[0,1], W_sum[1,1], ... - Consecutive threads read consecutive elements for fixed c_in. - But the loop is over c_in, so in iteration c_in, all threads read W_sum[c_in, c_out] for their respective c_out. - If W_sum is stored as [C_in, C_out] in row-major, W_sum[c_in, c_out] is at offset c_in*C_out + c_out. - Thread c_out reads offset c_in*C_out + c_out. - Consecutive threads read consecutive memory! Perfect coalescing! This is excellent. Similarly for the other weight tensors. So in a single kernel, per batch block: 1. For c_in = 0..63: a. Read input channel (b, c_in) and compute stats (4 values). b. Broadcast stats to all threads. c. Each thread c_out reads 4 weight values and updates its accumulator. 2. Finalize and write output. This is very efficient! No global memory writes for intermediate stats. Let me design this kernel. ```cuda __global__ void fused_conv_transpose_mean_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ W_sum, const float* __restrict__ W_top, const float* __restrict__ W_left, const float* __restrict__ w00, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, float multiplier ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c_out = threadIdx.x; int hw = H * W; int h2w2 = 4 * hw; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { const float* ptr = input + ((b * C_in + c_in) * hw); // Compute stats for this channel float total = 0.0f; float row0 = 0.0f; float col0 = 0.0f; float corner = 0.0f; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < hw; idx += blockDim.x) { float val = ptr[idx]; total += val; if (idx < W) row0 += val; if ((idx & (W - 1)) == 0) col0 += val; if (idx == 0) corner = val; } // Reduce within block __shared__ float s_total; __shared__ float s_row0; __shared__ float s_col0; __shared__ float s_corner; // Use warp shuffle for reduction first total = warp_reduce_sum(total); row0 = warp_reduce_sum(row0); col0 = warp_reduce_sum(col0); corner = warp_reduce_sum(corner); // only one thread has it, but reduce anyway if (threadIdx.x % 32 == 0) { // Store to shared // But we have 128 threads = 4 warps. Need cross-warp reduction too. } } } ``` Wait, the problem is that all 128 threads are computing the same stats simultaneously (since they all iterate over c_in together). We only need to compute stats once per c_in, not once per thread. Let me restructure: - All 128 threads cooperatively compute stats for the current c_in. - But then each thread uses the stats independently for its c_out. Yes, that's what I need. The inner loop over idx should use all threads. ```cuda // Phase 1: all threads compute stats for c_in float total = 0.0f, row0 = 0.0f, col0 = 0.0f, corner = 0.0f; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < hw; idx += blockDim.x) { float val = ptr[idx]; total += val; if (idx < W) row0 += val; if ((idx & (W - 1)) == 0) col0 += val; if (idx == 0) corner = val; } ``` Then reduce all threads' partial sums to a single value. Let me write the final code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_src = r''' __global__ void fused_conv_transpose_mean_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ W_sum, const float* __restrict__ W_top, const float* __restrict__ W_left, const float* __restrict__ w00, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, float multiplier ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int hw = H * W; float norm = 4.0f * (float)hw; bool valid_out = (tid < C_out); float acc = 0.0f; extern __shared__ float sdata[]; int nt = blockDim.x; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { const float* ptr = input + ((b * C_in + c_in) * hw); float total = 0.0f; float row0 = 0.0f; float col0 = 0.0f; float corner = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < hw; idx += nt) { float val = ptr[idx]; total += val; if (idx < W) row0 += val; if ((idx % W) == 0) col0 += val; if (idx == 0) corner = val; } sdata[tid] = total; sdata[nt + tid] = row0; sdata[2 * nt + tid] = col0; sdata[3 * nt + tid] = corner; __syncthreads(); for (int s = nt / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + s]; sdata[nt + tid] += sdata[nt + tid + s]; sdata[2 * nt + tid] += sdata[2 * nt + tid + s]; sdata[3 * nt + tid] += sdata[3 * nt + tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } float s_total = sdata[0]; float s_row0 = sdata[nt]; float s_col0 = sdata[2 * nt]; float s_corner = sdata[3 * nt]; if (valid_out) { int w_idx = c_in * C_out + tid; acc += s_total * W_sum[w_idx] - s_row0 * W_top[w_idx] - s_col0 * W_left[w_idx] + s_corner * w00[w_idx]; } __syncthreads(); } if (valid_out) { float result = (acc / norm + bias[tid]) * multiplier; output[b * C_out + tid] = result; } } ''' cpp_src = r''' torch::Tensor fused_conv_transpose_mean( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor W_sum, torch::Tensor W_top, torch::Tensor W_left, torch::Tensor w00, torch::Tensor bias, float multiplier ) { int B = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int C_out = bias.size(0); auto output = torch::empty({B, C_out}, input.options()); int threads = 256; int blocks = B; int smem = 4 * threads * sizeof(float); return output; } ''' fused_conv_transpose_mean = load_inline( name="fused_conv_transpose_mean", cuda_sources=cuda_src, cpp_sources=cpp_src, functions=["fused_conv_transpose_mean"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, multiplier): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d( in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding ) self.multiplier = multiplier # Validate parameters match our optimized assumption assert stride == 2 and kernel_size == 3 and padding == 1 and output_padding == 1, \ "Optimized kernel only supports stride=2, kernel=3, padding=1, output_padding=1" def forward(self, x): x = x.contiguous() B, C_in, H, W = x.shape C_out = self.conv_transpose.out_channels w = self.conv_transpose.weight bias = self.conv_transpose.bias W_sum = w.sum(dim=[2, 3]).contiguous() W_top = w[:, :, 0, :].sum(dim=2).contiguous() W_left = w[:, :, :, 0].sum(dim=2).contiguous() w00 = w[:, :, 0, 0].contiguous() out = fused_conv_transpose_mean(x, W_sum, W_top, W_left, w00, bias, self.multiplier) return out.view(B, C_out, 1, 1) ``` Wait, I just noticed: the `name` parameter in `load_inline` is used for the module name. reasoning
The compilation error is happening because the `cuda_sources` and `cpp_sources` are being placed in different files by `load_inline`. The `cpp_sources` goes into `main.cpp` and `cuda_sources` goes into `cuda.cu`. The C++ file tries to reference the CUDA kernel but can't see it. One solution is to put everything in `cuda_sources`. Another common approach is to put the kernel declaration in `cuda_sources` and then call it from `cpp_sources` if we declare it as `extern`. But actually, let me check if we can put both the `.cu` kernel and the C++ wrapper in the same `cuda_sources` string. Actually, the issue is that each source string gets its own file. The typical approach with `load_inline` is to include the C++ headers in the CUDA source too, or put everything in `cpp_sources`. Let me look at this differently. A common pattern is: ```python load_inline( name="...", cpp_sources=cpp_src, # C++ code cuda_sources=cuda_src, # CUDA code ... ) ``` And the CUDA code typically declares the kernel with `extern "C"` or just as a regular CUDA function, and the C++ code needs to see its declaration. A common approach is to put a forward declaration in `cpp_sources` or use `extern "C"` to prevent name mangling. Actually, looking at PyTorch's load_inline documentation, a simpler approach is to just put everything in `cuda_sources` - both the kernel and the wrapper function. The CUDA compiler can compile both `.cu` and `.cpp` code. Let me try that.

reasoning
The issue is that load_inline creates a main.cpp from the cpp_sources, but it ALSO compiles cuda.cu from the cuda_sources. However, the C++ wrapper function `fused_forward` in cuda.cu can't be seen from main.cpp because C++ can't see __global__ functions declared in .cu files when compiling .cpp files. Looking at the error more closely: main.cpp is auto-generated by load_inline and it tries to call `torch::wrap_pybind_function(fused_forward)`. But main.cpp is compiled as a regular C++ file (not CUDA), so it can't see the C++ torch::Tensor function defined in the .cu file because C++ compiled .cpp can't see declarations from .cu files unless they're properly declared. The solution is to put the C++ wrapper function in `cpp_sources` and declare it properly. But calling `<<<>>>` from a .cpp file requires that the kernel function is declared as `extern "C"` or there's a host-side wrapper in the .cu that calls it. Let me use a different approach: declare the kernel launcher in cpp_sources as an `extern` function implemented in the cuda_sources. Or better yet, provide a proper header-like declaration. Actually, looking at the PyTorch docs and examples for load_inline, a common pattern is: - cuda_sources: contains the CUDA kernels with `extern "C"` and the launching wrapper that returns void or the result - cpp_sources: contains the C++ bindings that call the wrapper The simplest fix is to make the kernel function `extern "C"` and put a host wrapper in the cuda_sources that returns a tensor, then declare that host wrapper as `extern` in the cpp_sources and call wrap_pybind_function on it. Or even simpler: look at how load_inline is designed. The `cpp_sources` are meant to contain the C++ code that wraps PyTorch tensors and calls CUDA. The `cuda_sources` just contain the CUDA device code. But actually, the .cu extension is compiled with nvcc, and main.cpp is compiled with g++. The issue is that the C++ function `fused_forward` in cuda.cu needs to be visible to main.cpp. Standard practice: declare an `extern "C"` wrapper in the cuda.cu that takes raw pointers and is declared in the cpp.cpp, or put both the kernel and the C++ launching code in cpp_sources and see if nvcc handles it. Actually, wait — I think we can put CUDA code inside cpp_sources if we name the file appropriately, or use `extra_cflags` to compile cpp_sources with nvcc. But that's complex. The cleanest approach: have `cuda_sources` define the kernel + an `extern "C"` function that calls it, and have `cpp_sources` declare that extern function and bind it with pybind11: ```cpp // cuda_sources __global__ void my_kernel(...) { ... } extern "C" void launch_my_kernel(args...) { my_kernel<<<...>>>(...); } // cpp_sources extern "C" void launch_my_kernel(args...); // Then wrap it in a torch::Tensor C++ function and bind that. ``` But there's an even simpler approach: just use `cpp_sources` for BOTH the host wrapper and the pybind, and put a forward declaration of the kernel in there too. But g++ can't parse `__global__`. OK here's what actually works based on standard load_inline examples: ```python cuda_src = ''' __global__ void kernel(...) {...} ''' cpp_src = ''' void launch_kernel(...) { kernel<<<...>>>(...); } torch::Tensor my_func(torch::Tensor x) { ... launch_kernel(...); return x; } ''' ``` But the issue is `kernel<<<>>>` in cpp_src .cpp compilation fails because g++ doesn't understand `<<<>>>`. So the CUDA kernel launch must happen in a `.cu` file. We need the `.cu` file to contain a C-linkage or C++ function that launches the kernel AND returns a tensor, and the `.cpp` file needs to see that function. Actually, looking at the compilation output, the `.cu` file IS compiled. The issue is just that `fused_forward` defined in `cuda.cu` can't be seen from `main.cpp`. We need to provide a declaration. Let me add a declaration of `fused_forward` in `cpp_sources`: ```cpp torch::Tensor fused_forward(torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor, float); PYBIND11_MODULE(...) { ... } ``` Wait but if we put that declaration, then when main.cpp compiles it can find the declaration. Then the linker will link it with the object from cuda.cu. Let's try this! Also wait, load_inline auto-generates the pybind11 module from `functions=[...]`. We should NOT provide our own PYBIND11_MODULE. We just need to declare the functions we want to bind so that main.cpp can see them. Actually load_inline puts the declarations and pybind calls in main.cpp. If `fused_forward` is defined in cuda.cu, then main.cpp just needs the declaration. But does load_inline do that? Looking at the error, it seems like main.cpp just tries to use `fused_forward` without declaration: ```cpp m.def("fused_forward", torch::wrap_pybind_function(fused_forward), "fused_forward"); ``` So we just need to add a forward declaration in cpp_sources. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncuda_src = r'''\n__global__ void fused_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ input,\n const float* __restrict__ W_sum,\n const float* __restrict__ bias,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int B, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out,\n float multiplier\n) {\n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n int nt = blockDim.x;\n int hw = H * W;\n float out_hw = 4.0f * (float)hw;\n \n extern __shared__ float s_sum[];\n \n // Compute sum of input over spatial dims for each c_in\n for (int c = tid; c < C_in; c += nt) {\n const float* ptr = input + b * C_in * hw + c * hw;\n float s = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll 4\n for (int i = 0; i < hw; i++) {\n s += ptr[i];\n }\n s_sum[c] = s;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n // Now compute output for each c_out\n #pragma unroll 8\n for (int c_out = tid; c_out < C_out; c_out += nt) {\n float acc = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll 8\n for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) {\n acc += s_sum[c_in] * W_sum[c_in * C_out + c_out];\n }\n output[b * C_out + c_out] = (acc / out_hw + bias[c_out]) * multiplier;\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor fused_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor W_sum,\n torch::Tensor bias,\n float multiplier\n) {\n int B = input.size(0);\n int C_in = input.size(1);\n int H = input.size(2);\n int W = input.size(3);\n int C_out = bias.size(0);\n \n auto output = torch::empty({B, C_out}, input.options());\n \n int threads = 256;\n int blocks = B;\n int smem = C_in * sizeof(float);\n \n fused_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n W_sum.data_ptr<float>(),\n bias.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, C_in, H, W, C_out,\n multiplier\n );\n \n return output;\n}\n'''\n\ncpp_src = r'''\ntorch::Tensor fused_forward(torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor, float);\n'''\n\nmodule = load_inline(\n name=\"fused_conv_v4\",\n cuda_sources=cuda_src,\n cpp_sources=cpp_src,\n functions=[\"fused_forward\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, multiplier):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(\n in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size,\n stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding\n )\n self.multiplier = multiplier\n \n def forward(self, x):\n x = x.contiguous()\n B, C_in, H, W = x.shape\n C_out = self.conv_transpose.out_channels\n \n w = self.conv_transpose.weight\n bias = self.conv_transpose.bias\n \n W_sum = w.sum(dim=[2, 3]).contiguous() # C_in x C_out\n \n out = module.fused_forward(x, W_sum, bias, self.multiplier)\n return out.view(B, C_out, 1, 1)\n" }

Since output is 2H x 2W and input H x W, each output pixel has specific inclusion pattern. For output size 2H x 2W: The even rows (0,2,4,...) receive fewer contributions from edge pixels. The average is a complex combination. Let me think about a 1D example. I'm getting into a complex simplification problem. Let me take a different approach: Since the final output is just B x C_out x 1 x 1, and the input is 128x128, what if I precompute what transformation matrix we need? Actually, for any linear transformation y = Wx + b followed by averaging to scalar, it's equivalent to y_scalar = (sum of all rows of W) · x / n_elements + b. For the conv transpose, if we reshape W to a matrix of size (256*256*C_out) x (128*128*C_in), the average over output is: (1'_{256*256} ⊗ I_{C_out})^T W x / (256*256). This is a linear functional on x. So the equivalent matrix for the combined operation is: W_eff = (1'_{256*256} ⊗ I_{C_out})^T W / (256*256) This simplifies to computing, for each output channel c_out and input spatial position (h,w) and input channel c_in: sum_{(i,j) in output} W'[(i,j), c_out, (h,w), c_in] / (256*256) where W' is the implicit conv transpose matrix. For stride=2, input at (h,w) connects to output positions: (2h, 2w), (2h, 2w+1), (2h+1, 2w), (2h+1, 2w+1) with various kernel weights. Actually let me use proper indexing. The conv transpose input-output relation: for c_out, c_in: output at position (i,j) gets sum over kernel position (a,b): input[(i + padding - a) / stride, (j + padding - b) / stride, c_in] * W[c_out, c_in, a, b] when the division yields integer and bounds are met. For stride=2, padding=1, to get output position (i,j): We look for a,b such that (i + 1 - a) is even and 0 <= (i+1-a)/2 < H, and similarly for j. Output position i is in [0, 2H-1], j in [0, 2W-1]. For a given output (i,j): Valid a values: a = i+1 - 2h for h in [0, H-1], so a in {i+1, i-1, i-3, ...} intersect [0,2] For each valid a where i+1-a is even and non-negative. For i=0: a in {1} (since 0+1-2*0=1, a=1) For i=1: a in {0,2} (1+1-2*0=2(a=2?), 1+1-2*1=0(a=0)) For i=2: a in {1} (2+1-2*1=1) For i=3: a in {0,2} (3+1-2*1=2, 3+1-2*2=0) Pattern: odd i -> a=1, even i -> a=0 or 2 Similarly for j. So each output position receives contributions from a small number of input positions with specific kernel weights. For the average, we need: for each c_out and each input position (h,w,c_in): sum contributions over all output (i,j) that connect to (h,w). sizeof(float);\n \n fused_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n W_sum_all.data_ptr<float>(),\n W_top_row.data_ptr<float>(),\n W_left_col.data_ptr<float>(),\n PyTorch ConvTranspose2d with padding: the input is effectively dilated with zeros between elements, then a standard convolution with kernel_size and given padding is applied. For stride=2, dilation=1, padding=1: 1. Input is dilated: between each element, insert stride-1=1 zero. If input is n x n, dilated is (2n-1) x (2n-1). 2. For the standard conv output at (h, w), the input is dilated_in[h + k_h - 1, w + k_w - 1]. This is non-zero only when h + k_h - 1 is even and w + k_w - 1 is even. Actually, output_padding just changes the formula for computing output size. It doesn't add zero padding after the conv; it adjusts the deconvolution arithmetic so that the output is larger. reasoning
Looking at the output, each input pixel at position (ih, iw) contributes to a 3x3 block in the output at position (ih*2, iw*2), but the overlaps are quite complex. The output from (0,0) goes to rows [0,1] and cols [0,1] (2x2 block). The output from (0,1) goes to rows [0,1] and cols [1,2,3]. There's a column 1 overlap. For a general weight, it's quite complex. But for the sum over all output, we still have: sum_out = sum_out(g) = sum_{ih,iw,oh,ow} input[ih, iw] * f_weight(ih, iw, oh, ow) = sum_{ih, iw} input[ih, iw] * sum_{oh, ow} f_weight(ih, iw, oh, ow) = sum_{ih, iw} input[ih, iw] * W[ih, iw]_sum So the formula for the first mean (over output dims) is still correct if we define W[ih, iw]_sum properly. But after the first mean (which outputs shape [B, C_out, 1, 1]), the second mean over [2,3] does nothing (dims are already size 1)! Wait, looking at the original code: ```python x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2, 3], keepdim=True) # First global average pooling x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2, 3], keepdim=True) # Second global average pooling ``` After the first mean, x has shape [B, C_out, 1, 1]. The second mean over dims [2,3] of a [B, C_out, 1, 1] tensor gives the same result! Because mean of a single element is itself. So actually the second mean is a no-op. We just need to match the first mean. OK so our formula for the first mean should be correct, but we need to compute the correct weight sums. Let me verify using the simple example. For the weight case (all ones): - W[ih=0, iw=0]_sum = 4 (it's a 2x2 block of ones) -- from the output we see 4 ones in the top-left 2x2 for just input[0,0]=1 - W[ih=0, iw=1]_sum = 6? Let me check: input[0,1]=1 contributes to output rows 0,1 and cols 1,2,3. With weight=1, that's 6 ones. - W[ih=1, iw=0]_sum = 6 (rows 1,2,3 and cols 0,1) - W[ih=1, iw=1]_sum = 9 (rows 1,2,3 and cols 1,2,3) Sum = 4 + 6 + 6 + 9 = 25. And the actual output sum for all-ones input is 25. ✓ Now for our actual problem, the input is 128x128, weight is 3x3, stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1. The input (128,128) maps to output (256, 256). For each input pixel (ih, iw), the contribution is a 3x3 block at (ih*2, iw*2) in the output, but clipped by the 256x256 boundary. Each position in the 3x3 block has a different weight. So W[ih, iw]_sum = sum_{k=0}^{2} sum_{l=0}^{2} w[k,l] * valid(ih*2-1+k, iw*2-1+l) Where the output indices are from ih*2 - padding + k = ih*2 - 1 + k, and similarly for iw. Valid means 0 <= oh < 256 and 0 <= ow < 256. Since ih ranges from 0 to 127: oh = 2*ih - 1 + k For ih=0: oh = -1, 0, 1. Only 0, 1 are valid (2 out of 3) For ih=1: oh = 1, 2, 3. All valid (3) ... For ih=127: oh = 253, 254, 255. All valid (3) Similarly for iw. So for corner input pixels (ih=0 or iw=0): ih=0, iw=0: valid weights: only weights at (k,l) where oh>=0, ow>=0, i.e., k>=1, l>=1. So 4 weights: w[1,1], w[1,2], w[2,1], w[2,2] ih=0, iw>0: valid: k>=1, all l. So 6 weights: w[1,:] and w[2,:] ih>0, iw=0: valid: all k, l>=1. So 6 weights: w[:,1] and w[:,2] ih>0, iw>0: all 9 weights. This matches with the 4, 6, 6, 9 pattern I observed! So in our kernel, for each c_in and c_out, we need different weight sums for: - general positions (ih>0, iw>0): sum of all 9 weights - top edge (ih=0, iw>0): sum of w[1,:] + w[2,:] - left edge (ih>0, iw=0): sum of w[:,1] + w[:,2] - corner (ih=0, iw=0): sum of w[1,1] + w[1,2] + w[2,1] + w[2,2] Similarly for the bottom and right edges if they existed, but with padding=1 and output size exactly 2*input_size, the bottom/right edge doesn't need clipping since ih=127 gives oh=253,254,255 and 255 < 256. OK so the weight sums are: W_all = sum of all 9 weights W_top_edge = sum for ih=0, iw>0 = w[1,0]+w[1,1]+w[1,2]+w[2,0]+w[2,1]+w[2,2] W_left_edge = sum for ih>0, iw=0 = w[0,1]+w[1,1]+w[2,1]+w[0,2]+w[1,2]+w[2,2] W_corner = sum for ih=0, iw=0 = w[1,1]+w[1,2]+w[2,1]+w[2,2] Wait, let me verify the formula for the top edge. For ih=0: oh = -1 + k. Valid when k >= 1, so k in {1, 2}. Actually, the weight[0,0,0,0] corresponds to the "anchor" point of the kernel. Through transposed convolution, input pixels activate different regions of the output depending on their position. But the key insight is: for the SUM over all output positions, each input pixel (ih, iw) activates a set of output positions whose combined weights equal the correct sum.
