And W_top_edge = weight[:, 1:, :].sum() = 6 for all-ones (correct). And W_all = 9. So the formulas in terms of weight are: W_all = weight.sum(dim=[2,3]) W_top_edge = weight[:, :, 1:, :].sum(dim=[2,3]) W_left_edge = weight[:, :, :, 1:].sum(dim=[2,3]) W_corner = weight[:, :, 1:, 1:].sum(dim=[2,3]) Then differences: W_all - W_top_edge = weight[:, :, 0, :].sum() = W_top_row W_all - W_left_edge = weight[:, :, :, 0].sum() = W_left_col W_all - W_top_edge - W_left_edge + W_corner = weight[:, :, 0, 0] = W_00 And the result formula: result = sum_c_in [ total_sum * W_all - top_sum * W_top_row - left_sum * W_left_col + corner * W_00 ] for each c_out. Let me verify with all-ones: For c_in=0, c_out=0: total_sum = 4, top_sum = 2, left_sum = 2, corner = 1 W_all = 9, W_top_row = 3, W_left_col = 3, W_00 = 1 result = 4*9 - 2*3 - 2*3 + 1*1 = 36 - 6 - 6 + 1 = 25 ✓ Divide by output size (4*4=16): 25/16 = 1.5625 Actual output for all-ones: tensor([[1., 2., 1., 1.], [2., 4., 2., 2.], [1., 2., 1., 1.], [1., 2., 1., 1.]]) Mean = 25/16 = 1.5625 ✓ So the formula is CORRECT! Now in code, I need to precompute: W_all = weight.sum(dim=[2,3]) # [C_out, C_in] W_top_row = weight[:, :, 0, :].sum(dim=2) # [C_out, C_in] W_left_col = weight[:, :, :, 0].sum(dim=2) # [C_out, C_in] W_00 = weight[:, :, 0, 0] # [C_out, C_in] Then the kernel computes per batch, per channel: total_sum, top_sum, left_sum, corner And then per c_out: acc = sum_c_in [ total_sum[c_in] * W_all[c_out, c_in] - top_sum[c_in] * W_top_row[c_out, c_in] - left_sum[c_in] * W_left_col[c_out, c_in] + corner[c_in] * W_00[c_out, c_in] ] output = (acc / (4*H*W) + bias[c_out]) * multiplier Now I need to write a kernel that: 1. Computes the 4 input aggregates per channel (total_sum, top_sum, left_sum, corner) 2. Does the matrix multiplication with the weight aggregates For performance, I'll use shared memory to store the input aggregates, and have each thread compute multiple output channels. Actually, let me use a different approach: - Use block dimensions: let's say one block per batch element - Threads in block compute input aggregates collaboratively - Then compute output channels For the aggregate computation, we can have each thread sum a subset of spatial positions, then reduce. For total_sum: each thread sums some pixels, reduce in shared memory. For top_sum: same but only first row. For left_sum: same but only first column. For corner: just element (0,0). Actually, top_sum just needs a horizontal sum of the first row. left_sum needs a vertical sum of the first column. total_sum needs a sum of all elements. corner is a single element. For efficiency: - We can compute all 4 sums in a single pass over the data. - Each thread iterates over spatial positions and accumulates all 4 values. - Then reduce the 4 values separately. Let me write this kernel carefully. ```cuda __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* input, const float* W_all, const float* W_top_row, const float* W_left_col, const float* W_00, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, float multiplier ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nt = blockDim.x; int hw = H * W; // Shared memory layout // First C_in floats: total_sum per channel // Next C_in floats: top_sum per channel // Next C_in floats: left_sum per channel // Next C_in floats: corner per channel extern __shared__ float sdata[]; float* s_total = sdata; float* s_top = sdata + C_in; float* s_left = sdata + 2 * C_in; float* s_corner = sdata + 3 * C_in; // Reduction buffers for 4 aggregates // We use 4 * nt floats after the channel data float* s_red = sdata + 4 * C_in; #define R0 0 #define R1 nt #define R2 (2 * nt) #define R3 (3 * nt) // Each channel needs to be reduced for (int c = 0; c < C_in; c++) { const float* ptr = input + ((b * C_in) + c) * hw; float my_total = 0.0f; float my_top = 0.0f; float my_left = 0.0f; float my_corner = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < hw; idx += nt) { float val = ptr[idx]; my_total += val; if (idx < W) my_top += val; if (idx % W == 0) my_left += val; if (idx == 0) my_corner = val; } s_red[R0 + tid] = my_total; s_red[R1 + tid] = my_top; s_red[R2 + tid] = my_left; s_red[R3 + tid] = my_corner; __syncthreads(); // Reduce for (int s = nt / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { s_red[R0 + tid] += s_red[R0 + tid + s]; s_red[R1 + tid] += s_red[R1 + tid + s]; s_red[R2 + tid] += s_red[R2 + tid + s]; s_red[R3 + tid] += s_red[R3 + tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid == 0) { s_total[c] = s_red[R0]; s_top[c] = s_red[R1]; s_left[c] = s_red[R2]; s_corner[c] = s_red[R3]; } __syncthreads(); } // Now compute output float out_hw = 4.0f * hw; for (int c_out = tid; c_out < C_out; c_out += nt) { float acc = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 8 for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { int widx = c_out * C_in + c_in; // Note: weight is [C_out, C_in] acc += s_total[c_in] * W_all[widx] - s_top[c_in] * W_top_row[widx] - s_left[c_in] * W_left_col[widx] + s_corner[c_in] * W_00[widx]; } output[b * C_out + c_out] = (acc / out_hw + bias[c_out]) * multiplier; } } ``` Wait, weight indexing. In PyTorch, conv_transpose.weight has shape [C_out, C_in, K, K]. So W_all = weight.sum(dim=[2,3]) has shape [C_out, C_in], with W_all[c_out, c_in]. So in the kernel, widx = c_out * C_in + c_in. But in the PyTorch code, when we use `.permute(1, 0)`, the shape becomes [C_in, C_out]. So we need to be careful. Actually, let me NOT permute. Keep it as [C_out, C_in] and use widx = c_out * C_in + c_in. For contiguous memory access, the kernel accesses c_in sequentially for a fixed c_out. Accessing W_all[c_out, c_in] for c_in = 0, 1, 2, ... means accessing memory with stride 1 (since the inner dimension is c_in). That's good for coalescing! Actually wait, in the loop, we're varying c_in for fixed c_out. The memory layout is [c_out, c_in] row-major. So W_all[c_out * C_in + c_in] varies c_in, which IS contiguous. Good! Let me now verify the weight precomputation in Python: ```python w = self.conv_transpose.weight # [C_out, C_in, 3, 3] W_all = w.sum(dim=[2, 3]).contiguous() # [C_out, C_in] W_top_row = w[:, :, 0, :].sum(dim=2).contiguous() # [C_out, C_in] W_left_col = w[:, :, :, 0].sum(dim=2).contiguous() # [C_out, C_in] W_00 = w[:, :, 0, 0].contiguous() # [C_out, C_in] ``` Yes, this gives [C_out, C_in] for all of them. Now, one optimization: instead of storing 4 weight matrices, I could precompute a single combined matrix. But that might not save much memory bandwidth (we still need all the values). Actually, note that the formula is: acc = total * W_all - top * W_top_row - left * W_left_col + corner * W_00 = total * W_all + top * (-W_top_row) + left * (-W_left_col) + corner * W_00 = dot([total, top, left, corner], [W_all, -W_top_row, -W_left_col, W_00]) So I could precompute 4 weight matrices and do 4 dot products. Since C_in=64 and C_out=128, the memory is small and likely fits in L2 cache. So this should be fine. Actually, an even better optimization: precompute the 4 weight sums on the CPU or in a pre-processing step, so the kernel just reads them. For the kernel shared memory: 4 * C_in floats = 4 * 64 = 256 floats = 1024 bytes. That's tiny. Reduction buffer: 4 * nt floats = 4 * 256 = 1024 floats = 4096 bytes. Total shared memory: ~5KB. Very little. For the computation of output, each c_out does C_in=64 iterations. For C_out=128 channels and nt=256 threads, each thread does 128/256 = 0.5 channels on average. So roughly half the threads compute one c_out. Each such computation does 64 multiplications. That's very little work. To increase parallelism, I could use smaller thread blocks (e.g., 128 threads) and have more work per thread. Or I could have each thread compute multiple c_out channels. Actually, with 256 threads and only 128 output channels, half the threads are idle during the output computation. But the input reduction is parallelized across all 256 threads. Since H*W = 128*128 = 16384 pixels per channel, and we have 64 channels, each thread processes about 16384/256 = 64 pixels per channel. That's fine. Wait, but we do the reduction sequentially over channels (outer loop over c=0..63). Each channel processes 16384 pixels. For 64 channels, that's about 1M pixels total. At memory bandwidth of 3.35 TB/s, reading 4MB (1M floats) takes ~1.2 microseconds. But we have bank conflicts from the __syncthreads and the reduction operation might add overhead. For maximum occupancy, we'd want many concurrent thread blocks. With 16 batch elements and 256 threads per block, we have 16 blocks. On an H100 with many SMs, this might not fully utilize the GPU. But since each block does a lot of work (reduction over 1M pixels), it should be OK. Actually, let me think about a different approach: use a single warp per channel for reduction, and then have all warps compute all output channels. But given the small size, the current approach is probably fine. Let me write the final kernel and test. One more thing: I should make sure the weight precomputations are done efficiently. `w.sum(dim=[2,3])` should be fast. Let me write the code. Actually, I realize there might be an issue with the `w[:, :, 0, :].sum(dim=2)` syntax. Let me make sure: - `w[:, :, 0, :]` selects the first row of each kernel, shape [C_out, C_in, 3] - `.sum(dim=2)` sums over the last dimension (the columns), giving [C_out, C_in] Yes, that's correct. For `w[:, :, :, 0]`: - Shape [C_out, C_in, 3] - `.sum(dim=2)` sums over the last dimension (the rows), giving [C_out, C_in] Wait, `w[:, :, :, 0]` has shape [C_out, C_in, 3]. The last dimension (dim=2) is the row index. So `.sum(dim=2)` sums over rows, giving the sum of the first column. That's correct. For `w[:, :, 0, 0]`: - Shape [C_out, C_in] - No sum needed. For `w.sum(dim=[2,3])`: - Sums over dims 2 and 3, giving [C_out, C_in]. OK, let me write the code. Also, I need to make sure I handle the output shape correctly. The original returns [B, C_out, 1, 1]. Our kernel returns [B, C_out]. We reshape at the end. Let me also think about whether we need to handle non-unit batch sizes. The code uses B=16, but the kernel generalizes to any B. One more optimization: I can use float4 or vectorized loads for the input. But given the strided access pattern (with modular arithmetic for left_sum), vectorization might not help much. Let me write the code now.
